src="/w/load.php?debug=false&amp;lang=en&amp;modules=html5shiv&amp;only=scripts&amp;skin=vector&amp;sync=1"></script><![endif]-->\n</head>\n<body class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject page-Secularism rootpage-Secularism skin-vector action-view">\t\t<div id="mw-page-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Secularism</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p><b>Secularism</b>, as defined in the Miriam-Webster dictionary<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>, is the "indifference to, or rejection or exclusion of, religion and religious considerations." As a philosophy, secularism seeks to interpret life on principles taken solely from the material world, without recourse to religion<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>. In political terms, secularism is the principle of the separation of government institutions and persons <a href="/wiki/Mandate_(politics)" title="Mandate (politics)">mandated</a> to represent <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">the state</a> from <a href="/wiki/Religious_organization" title="Religious organization">religious institution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">religious dignitaries</a> (the attainment of such is termed <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secularity</a>). One manifestation of secularism is asserting the right to be free from religious rule and teachings, or, in a state declared to be neutral on matters of belief, from the imposition by government of religion or religious practices upon its people.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;Notes 1&#93;</a></sup> Another manifestation of secularism is the view that public activities and decisions, especially <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> ones, should be uninfluenced by religious beliefs or practices.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;Notes 2&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Secularism draws its intellectual roots from Greek and Roman philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>; from <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denis_Diderot" title="Denis Diderot">Denis Diderot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>; and from more recent <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethinkers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_G._Ingersoll" title="Robert G. Ingersoll">Robert Ingersoll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a>. It shifts the focus from religion to other \xe2\x80\x98temporal\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98this-worldly\xe2\x80\x99 things with emphasis on nature, reason, science, and development.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The purposes and arguments in support of secularism vary widely.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In European <a href="/wiki/Laicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Laicism">laicism</a>, it has been argued that secularism is a movement toward <a href="/wiki/Modernization" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernization">modernization</a>, and away from traditional religious values (also known as <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a>). This type of secularism, on a social or philosophical level, has often occurred while maintaining an official <a href="/wiki/State_church" class="mw-redirect" title="State church">state church</a> or other state support of religion. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, some argue that <a href="#State_secularism">state secularism</a> has served to a greater extent to protect religion and the religious from governmental interference, while secularism on a social level is less prevalent.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Overview"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Overview</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#State_secularism"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">State secularism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Secular_society"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Secular society</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Secular_ethics"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Secular ethics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#American_interpretation_of_secularism"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">American interpretation of secularism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Secularism_in_late_20th_century_political_philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Secularism in late 20th century political philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Organizations"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Organizations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holyoake2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Holyoake2.JPG/220px-Holyoake2.JPG" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/Holyoake2.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="236" data-file-height="318" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holyoake2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British</a> writer <a href="/wiki/George_Jacob_Holyoake" class="mw-redirect" title="George Jacob Holyoake">George Jacob Holyoake</a> (1817\xe2\x80\x931906) coined the term "secularism" in 1851<sup id="cite_ref-Holyoake_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holyoake-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alberico_Gentili.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/220px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/330px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg 2x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alberico_Gentili.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italian</a> law professor <a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Alberico Gentili</a> (1552\xe2\x80\x931608) first split secularism from <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic theology">Roman Catholic theology</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The term "secularism" was first used by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> writer <a href="/wiki/George_Jacob_Holyoake" class="mw-redirect" title="George Jacob Holyoake">George Jacob Holyoake</a> in 1851.<sup id="cite_ref-Holyoake_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holyoake-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Holyoake invented the term secularism to describe his views of promoting a social order separate from religion, without actively dismissing or criticizing religious belief. An <a href="/wiki/Agnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a> himself, Holyoake argued that "Secularism is not an argument against Christianity, it is one independent of it. It does not question the pretensions of Christianity; it advances others. Secularism does not say there is no light or guidance elsewhere, but maintains that there is light and guidance in secular truth, whose conditions and sanctions exist independently, and act forever. Secular knowledge is manifestly that kind of knowledge which is founded in this life, which relates to the conduct of this life, conduces to the welfare of this life, and is capable of being tested by the experience of this life."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Barry_Kosmin" class="mw-redirect" title="Barry Kosmin">Barry Kosmin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Study_of_Secularism_in_Society_and_Culture" title="Institute for the Study of Secularism in Society and Culture">Institute for the Study of Secularism in Society and Culture</a> breaks modern secularism into two types: hard and soft secularism.  According to Kosmin, "the hard secularist considers religious propositions to be <a href="/wiki/Epistemological" class="mw-redirect" title="Epistemological">epistemologically</a> illegitimate, warranted by neither reason nor experience."  However, in the view of soft secularism, "the attainment of absolute truth was impossible and therefore skepticism and tolerance should be the principle and overriding values in the discussion of science and religion."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The departure from reliance on religious faith to reason and science marks the beginning of the secularization of education and society in history. Among the earliest documentations of a secular form of thought is seen in the <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Charvaka</a> system of philosophy, which held <a href="/wiki/Direct_perception" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct perception">direct perception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, and conditional <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a> as proper sources of knowledge, and sought to reject the prevailing religious practices of that time.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> According to Domenic Marbaniang, Secularism emerged in the West with the establishment of reason over religious faith as human reason was gradually liberated from unquestioned subjection to the dominion of religion and superstition.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Secularism first appeared in the West in the classical philosophy and politics of ancient Greece, disappeared for a time after the fall of Greece, but resurfaced after a millennium and half in the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a>. He writes:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>An increasing confidence in human capabilities, reason, and progress, that emerged during the Italian Renaissance, together with an increasing distrust in organized and state supported religion during the Reformation, was responsible for the ushering of modernity during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, which brought all facets of human life including religion under the purview of reason and thus became responsible for the freeing of education, society, and state from the domination of religion; in other words, the development of modern secularism.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Harvey Cox explains that the Enlightenment hailed Nature as the "deep reality" that transcended the corrupted man-made institutions of men. Consequently, the rights of man were not considered as God-given but as the de facto benefits of Nature as revealed by Reason.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="State_secularism">State secularism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: State secularism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_state_religions.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_state_religions.svg/250px-Map_of_state_religions.svg.png" width="250" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_state_religions.svg/375px-Map_of_state_religions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_state_religions.svg/500px-Map_of_state_religions.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_state_religions.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>. <table>  <tbody><tr valign="top"> <td> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a></div> </td> <td><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span> </td> <td> <div class="legend" href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a></div> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Secular_state" title="Secular state">Secular state</a></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em title="Liberalism in Russia">Russia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Senegal" title="Liberalism in Senegal">Senegal</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Serbia" title="Liberalism in Serbia">Serbia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Slovakia" title="Liberalism in Slovakia">Slovakia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Slovenia" title="Liberalism in Slovenia">Slovenia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_radicalism_in_Spain" title="Liberalism and radicalism in Spain">Spain</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_South_Africa" title="Liberalism in South Africa">South Africa</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_South_Korea" title="Liberalism in South Korea">South Korea</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_centrism_in_Sweden" title="Liberalism and centrism in Sweden">Sweden</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_radicalism_in_Switzerland" title="Liberalism and radicalism in Switzerland">Switzerland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Taiwan" title="Liberalism in Taiwan">Taiwan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Thailand" title="Liberalism in Thailand">Thailand</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Tunisia" title="Liberalism in Tunisia">Tunisia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Turkey" title="Liberalism in Turkey">Turkey</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Ukraine" title="Liberalism in Ukraine">Ukraine</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Liberalism in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">United States</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Uruguay" title="Liberalism in Uruguay">Uruguay</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Venezuela)" title="Liberal Party (Venezuela)">Venezuela</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Zimbabwe" title="Liberalism in Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border: 1px solid #FFBF00;text-align:center;color:black;">Related topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-top:0;"><div class="plainlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Democratic_capitalism" title="Democratic capitalism">Democratic capitalism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Political_views_of_American_academics" title="Political views of American academics">Liberal bias in academia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Regressive_left" title="Regressive left">Regressive left</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Yellow_flag_waving.svg" class="image"><img alt="Yellow flag waving.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/15px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/22px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Yellow_flag_waving.svg/30px-Yellow_flag_waving.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="268" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Liberalism" title="Portal:Liberalism">Liberalism&#32;portal</a></li>\n<li><span style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg" class="image"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics&#32;portal</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Liberalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Liberalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Liberalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In political terms, secularism is a movement towards the separation of religion and government (often termed the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>).  This can refer to reducing ties between a government and a <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>, replacing laws based on scripture (such as <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Halakha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominionism">Dominionism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> law) with civil laws, and eliminating discrimination on the basis of religion.  This is said to add to democracy by protecting the rights of religious minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his <i>On Temporal Authority (1523),</i> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> argued for the division of the church and the state. He specified two distinct powers: <i>weltliches Regiment</i> (German word for \xe2\x80\x98the kingdom of the world,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98the State\xe2\x80\x99) and <i>geistliches Regiment</i> (German word for \xe2\x80\x98the kingdom of God,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98the Church\xe2\x80\x99) and argued that citizens need only subject to the ruler\'s edict as long as the edict conformed to God\'s divine will as revealed in the scriptures.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Berlinerblau" title="Jacques Berlinerblau">Jacques Berlinerblau</a> of the Program for Jewish Civilization at the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Foreign_Service" title="School of Foreign Service">Edmund A. Walsh School of Foreign Service</a> at <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">Georgetown University</a>, have argued that the separation of church and state is but one possible strategy to be deployed by secular governments. What all secular governments, from the democratic to the authoritarian, share is a concern about the relationship between the church and the state. Each secular government may find its own unique policy prescriptions for dealing with that concern (separation being one of those possible policies; French models, in which the state carefully monitors and regulates the church, being another).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A major impact on the idea of state religious liberty came from the writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> who, in his <a href="/wiki/A_Letter_Concerning_Toleration" title="A Letter Concerning Toleration">A Letter Concerning Toleration</a> argued in favor of religious toleration. He argued that government must treat all citizens and all religions equally and that it can restrict actions but not the religious intent behind them.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maharaja_Ranjeet_Singh" class="mw-redirect" title="Maharaja Ranjeet Singh">Maharaja Ranjeet Singh</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikh empire">Sikh empire</a> of the first half of the 19th century successfully established a <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh#Secular_Sikh_rule" title="Ranjit Singh">secular rule</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>. This secular rule respected members of all races and religions and it allowed them to participate without discrimination in Ranjeet Singh\'s <a href="/wiki/Durbar_(court)" title="Durbar (court)">darbar</a> and he had Sikh, Muslim and Hindu representatives heading the darbar.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Ranjit Singh also extensively funded education, religion, and arts of various different religions and languages.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Secularism is most often associated with the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> in Europe and it plays a major role in <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western society</a>. The principles, but not necessarily the practices, of <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state_in_the_United_States" title="Separation of church and state in the United States">separation of church and state in the United States</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/La%C3%AFcit%C3%A9" title="La\xc3\xafcit\xc3\xa9">La\xc3\xafcit\xc3\xa9</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> draw heavily on secularism. Secular states also existed in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic world</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_secularism" title="Islam and secularism">Islam and secularism</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due in part to the belief in the separation of church and state, secularists tend to prefer that politicians make decisions for secular rather than religious reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In this respect, policy decisions pertaining to topics like <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a>, <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cell" title="Embryonic stem cell">embryonic stem cell</a> research, <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sex_education" title="Sex education">sex education</a> are prominently focused upon by American secularist organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Inquiry" title="Center for Inquiry">Center for Inquiry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Christian_fundamentalism" title="Christian fundamentalism">Christian fundamentalists</a> and scholars (notably in the United States) oppose secularism, often claiming that there is a "radical secularist" ideology being adopted in our current day and they see secularism as a threat to "Christian rights"<sup id="cite_ref-christianpost-jerrys-kids_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-christianpost-jerrys-kids-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and national security.<sup id="cite_ref-positiveatheism-foulwel_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-positiveatheism-foulwel-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This could be a sign that \xe2\x80\x98hard secularism\xe2\x80\x99 is taking over from \xe2\x80\x98soft secularism\xe2\x80\x99 towards becoming the predominant ideological mindset. According to the terminologi of Barry Kosmin, described earlier in this article.\n</p><p>The most significant forces of religious fundamentalism in the contemporary world are <a href="/wiki/Christian_fundamentalism" title="Christian fundamentalism">Christian fundamentalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Islamic fundamentalism</a>.  At the same time, one significant stream of secularism has come from religious minorities who see governmental and political secularism as integral to the preservation of equal rights.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the well known states that are often considered "<a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitutionally</a> secular" are the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> although none of these nations have identical forms of governance with respect to religion.\n</p><p>Countries with state religion in northern Europe have a high degree of political secularism with systems build upon protestant and democratic ideology. For example the monarchy of Denmark have a constitutional right for the freedom of religion, freedom of speech and it is illegal to discriminate individually upon religion, ethnic origins etc. \n</p><p>One of the most wellknown countries with a religious political system is the Islamic republic of Iran.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secular_society">Secular society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Secular society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In studies of religion, modern <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracies</a> are generally recognized as secular. This is due to the near-complete <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a> (beliefs on religion generally are not subject to legal or social sanctions), and the lack of authority of religious leaders over political decisions.  Nevertheless, it has been claimed that surveys done by <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> show Americans as generally being more comfortable with religion playing a major role in public life, while in Europe the impact of the church on public life is declining.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a> has, since <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a>, often been preoccupied with the problem of <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> in secularized societies and with <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a> as a sociological or historical process.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Twentieth-century scholars, whose work has contributed to the understanding of these matters, include <a href="/wiki/Carl_L._Becker" title="Carl L. Becker">Carl L. Becker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_L%C3%B6with" title="Karl L\xc3\xb6with">Karl L\xc3\xb6with</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Blumenberg" title="Hans Blumenberg">Hans Blumenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meyer_Howard_Abrams" class="mw-redirect" title="Meyer Howard Abrams">M. H. Abrams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_L._Berger" title="Peter L. Berger">Peter L. Berger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_B%C3%A9nichou" title="Paul B\xc3\xa9nichou">Paul B\xc3\xa9nichou</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=D._L._Munby&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="D. L. Munby (page does not exist)">D. L. Munby</a>, among others.\n</p><p>Some societies become increasingly secular as the result of social processes, rather than through the actions of a dedicated secular movement; this process is known as <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularization</a>.\n</p><p>Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Peter_L._Berger" title="Peter L. Berger">Peter L. Berger</a> maintained that the modern world can no longer be described as being secular or becoming increasingly secular, instead it can best be described as being pluralistic. <sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secular_ethics">Secular ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Secular ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Secular_ethics" title="Secular ethics">Secular ethics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secular_religion" title="Secular religion">Secular religion</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/George_Holyoake" title="George Holyoake">George Holyoake</a>\'s 1896 publication <i>English Secularism</i> describes secularism as follows:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>\nSecularism is a code of duty pertaining to this life, founded on considerations purely human, and intended mainly for those who find theology indefinite or inadequate, unreliable or unbelievable. Its essential principles are three: (1) The improvement of this life by material means. (2) That science is the available Providence of man. (3) That it is good to do good. Whether there be other good or not, the good of the present life is good, and it is good to seek that good.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Holyoake held that secularism and <a href="/wiki/Secular_ethics" title="Secular ethics">secular ethics</a> should take no interest at all in religious questions (as they were irrelevant), and was thus to be distinguished from strong <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethought</a> and atheism. In this he disagreed with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradlaugh" title="Charles Bradlaugh">Charles Bradlaugh</a>, and the disagreement split the secularist movement between those who argued that anti-religious movements and activism was not necessary or desirable and those who argued that it was.\n</p><p>Contemporary ethical debate in the West is often described as "secular." The work of well known moral philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Derek_Parfit" title="Derek Parfit">Derek Parfit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Singer" title="Peter Singer">Peter Singer</a>, and even the whole field of contemporary bioethics, have been described as explicitly secular or non-religious.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="American_interpretation_of_secularism">American interpretation of secularism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: American interpretation of secularism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>It has been argued that the concept of secularism has frequently been misinterpreted.<sup id="cite_ref-uooavbjacq_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uooavbjacq-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In a July 2012 <i><a href="/wiki/Huffington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Huffington Post">Huffington Post</a></i> article titled <i>Secularism Is Not Atheism</i>, Jacques Berlinerblau, Director of the Program for Jewish Civilization at Georgetown University, wrote that "Secularism must be the most misunderstood and mangled ism in the American political lexicon. Commentators on the right and the left routinely equate it with Stalinism, Nazism and Socialism, among other dreaded isms. In the United States, of late, another false equation has emerged. That would be the groundless association of secularism with atheism. The religious right has profitably promulgated this misconception at least since the 1970s."<sup id="cite_ref-uooavbjacq_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uooavbjacq-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secularism_in_late_20th_century_political_philosophy">Secularism in late 20th century political philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Secularism in late 20th century political philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>\nIt can be seen by many of the organizations (NGO\'s) for secularism that they prefer to define <i>secularism</i> as the common ground for all <a href="/wiki/Life_stance" title="Life stance">life stance</a> groups, religious or atheistic, to thrive in a society that honors freedom of speech and conscience. An example of that is the <a href="/wiki/National_Secular_Society" title="National Secular Society">National Secular Society</a> in the UK.  This is a common understanding of what secularism stands for among many of its activists throughout the world. However, many scholars of Christianity and conservative politicians seem to interpret secularism more often than not, as an antithesis of religion and an attempt to push religion out of society and replace it with <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a> or a void of values, <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">nihilism</a>. This dual aspect (as noted above in "Secular ethics") has created difficulties in political discourse on the subject. It seems that most political theorists in philosophy following the landmark work of <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">John Rawl</a>\xc2\xb4s <i>Theory of Justice</i> in 1971 and its following book, <i><a href="/wiki/Political_Liberalism" title="Political Liberalism">Political Liberalism</a></i> (1993),<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> would rather use the conjoined concept <i>overlapping consensus</i> rather than secularism. In the latter Rawls holds the idea of an overlapping consensus as one of three main ideas of <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">political liberalism</a>. He argues that the term <i>secularism</i> cannot apply; </p><blockquote><p>But what is a secular argument? Some think of any argument that is reflective and critical, publicly intelligible and rational, as a secular argument; [...], Nevertheless, a central feature of political liberalism is that it views all such arguments the same way it views religious ones, and therefore these secular philosophical doctrines do not provide public reasons. Secular concepts and reasoning of this kind belong to <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">first philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">moral doctrine</a>, and fall outside the domain of the political.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Still, Rawl\xc2\xb4s theory is akin to Holyoake\xc2\xb4s vision of a tolerant democracy that treats all <a href="/wiki/Life_stance" title="Life stance">life stance</a> groups alike. Rawl\xc2\xb4s idea it that it is in everybody\xc2\xb4s own interest to endorse "a reasonable constitutional democracy" with "principles of toleration". His work has been highly influential on scholars in political philosophy and his term, <i>overlapping consensus</i>, seems to have for many parts replaced <i>secularism</i> among them. In textbooks on modern political philosophy, like Colin Farelly\xc2\xb4s, <i>An Introduction to Contemporary Political Theory</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>  and Will Kymlicka\xc2\xb4s, <i>Contemporary Political Philosophy</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> the term secularism is not even indexed and in the former it can be seen only in one footnote. However, there is no shortage of discussion and coverage of the topic it involves. It is just called <i>overlapping consensus, <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(philosophy)" title="Pluralism (philosophy)">pluralism</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multiculturalism</a></i> or expressed in some other way. In The <i>Oxford Handbook of Political Theory</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> there is one chapter called "Political secularism", by <a href="/wiki/Rajeev_Bhargava" title="Rajeev Bhargava">Rajeev Bhargava</a>. It covers secularism in a global context and starts with this sentence: "Secularism is a beleaguered doctrine."\n</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizations">Organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Secularism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_secularist_organizations" title="List of secularist organizations">List of secularist organizations</a></div>\n<p>Groups such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Secular_Society" title="National Secular Society">National Secular Society</a> (United Kingdom) and <a href="/wiki/Americans_United" class="mw-redirect" title="Americans United">Americans United</a> campaign for secularism are often supported by <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">Humanists</a>. In 2005, the National Secular Society held the inaugural "Secularist of the Year" awards ceremony. The award\'s first winner was <a href="/wiki/Maryam_Namazie" title="Maryam Namazie">Maryam Namazie</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Worker-Communist_Party_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Worker-Communist Party of Iran">Worker-Communist Party of Iran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Central_Council_of_Ex-Muslims#Council_of_Ex-Muslims_of_Britain" title="Central Council of Ex-Muslims">Council of Ex-Muslims of Britain</a><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> which aims to break the taboo that comes with renouncing Islam and to oppose apostasy laws and political Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc-2007_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-2007-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Secular_Society" title="Scottish Secular Society">Scottish Secular Society</a> is active in Scotland and is currently focused on the role of religion in education. In 2013 it raised a petition at the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> to have the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Education_(Scotland)_Act_1980&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Education (Scotland) Act 1980 (page does not exist)">Education (Scotland) Act 1980</a>  changed so that parents will have to make a positive choice to opt into <a href="/wiki/Religious_Observance" title="Religious Observance">Religious Observance</a>.\n</p><p>Another secularist organization is the <a href="/wiki/Secular_Coalition_for_America" title="Secular Coalition for America">Secular Coalition for America</a>. The Secular Coalition for America lobbies and advocates for separation of church and state as well as the acceptance and inclusion of Secular Americans in American life and public policy.  While Secular Coalition for America is linked to many secular humanistic organizations and many <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">secular humanists</a> support it, as with the Secular Society, some non-humanists support it.\n</p><p>Local organizations work to raise the profile of secularism in their communities and tend to include secularists, freethinkers, atheists, agnostics, and humanists under their organizational umbrella.\n</p><p>Student organizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Secular_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="Toronto Secular Alliance">Toronto Secular Alliance</a>, try to popularize nontheism and secularism on campus.  The <a href="/wiki/Secular_Student_Alliance" title="Secular Student Alliance">Secular Student Alliance</a> is an educational nonprofit that organizes and aids such high school and college secular student groups.\n</p><p>In Turkey, the most prominent and active secularist organization is <a href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrkist_Thought_Association" title="Atat\xc3\xbcrkist Thought Association">Atat\xc3\xbcrkist Thought Association</a> (ADD), which is credited for organizing the <a href="/wiki/Republic_Protests" title="Republic Protests">Republic Protests</a> \xe2\x80\x93 demonstrations in the four largest cities in Turkey in 2007, where over 2 million people, mostly women, defended their concern in and support of secularist principles introduced by <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atat\xc3\xbcrk">Mustafa Kemal Atat\xc3\xbcrk</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leicester_Secular_Society" title="Leicester Secular Society">Leicester Secular Society</a> founded in 1851 is the world\'s oldest secular society.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
